The Obligation of Proof and the Exception for the Proven Authority
Maturidi defines the exception for following a proven authority and argues for the obligation of proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the exception to the prohibition of taqlid: following is only allowed when the person being followed has a compelling proof, and that each individual is obligated to know the truth through evidence.

Core Idea
Only those with proof deserve to be followed; everyone must seek truth through evidence.

After establishing that blind following is invalid, Imam Maturidi clarifies the only situation where following is acceptable: when the person being followed is himself a proof, with a demonstration that compels the impartial. He then emphasizes that every individual has the duty to know the truth in his religion, as if the evidence were present before him. Finally, he argues that true proofs cannot be multiple and contradictory, so only one path can be correct.

Guided Path
1
Read the exception clause
Read from 'اللهم إلا أن يكون الأحد' to the end. Notice the condition for valid following.
2
Understand the condition
The followed person must be a proof (حجة) with actions showing truthfulness and a demonstration (برهان) that compels impartial observers.
This is the only valid form of following.
3
Grasp the obligation of knowledge
Each person is required to know the truth in what he professes, as if the evidence is present. This implies personal intellectual effort.
4
See the argument against multiple truths
If multiple groups had equal proofs, their proofs would contradict each other, which is impossible. So only one can have the true proof.
This reinforces that truth is singular and must be sought via evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الحمد لله الذي كل حمد حمد به من دونه فراجع بالحقيقة إليه، حمدا يوافى نعمه ويكافئ من يده ويبلغ رضاه، ونسأله أن يصلى على من ختم به الرسالة وعلى إخوانه من المرسلين وعلى أوليائه أجمعين، ونستعصم به من الزلل ونرغب إليه فيا يكرمنا من القول والعمل
إبطال التقليد ووجوب معرفة الدين بالدليل
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: أما بعد، فإنا وجدنا الناس مختلفي المذاهب في النحل في الدين، متفقين على اختلافهم في الدين على كلمة واحدة: أن كلا عليه حق، والذي عليه غيره باطل، على اتفاق جملتهم الذي هو له سلف يُقلد، فثبت أن التقليد ليس مما يُعذر صاحبه لإصابة مثله منهم ضده، على أنه ليس فيه سوى كثرة العدد، اللهم إلا أن يكون الأحد ممن ينتهى القول إليه حجة عمل يعلم [بها] صدقه فيما يدعى، وبرهان يقهر المنصفين على إصابته الحق، فمن إليه مرجعه فى الدين بما يوجب تحقيقه عنه فهو المحق، وعلى كل واحد منهم معرفة الحق فيما يدين به، كأن الذي دان به هو مع [أدلة صاله؟] وشهادة الحق له - قد حضرهم؛ إذ منتهى حجج كل منهم التسليم له لو ظفر بها، وقد ظهرت لمن ذكرت، ولا يجوز ظهور مثلها لعدة في الدين؛ لما يتناقض حجج وأظهر تمويه أسباب الشبه في غيره، ولا قوة إلا بالله العظيم.
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Praise be to God, to Whom every praise that is praised by others ultimately returns in reality – praise that matches His blessings, suffices from His hand, and attains His pleasure. And we ask Him to bless the one with whom He sealed the message, and his brothers among the messengers, and all His saints. And we seek His protection from error and desire from Him that He honor us in speech and deed.
Refutation of taqlid (blind following) and the obligation of knowing religion by proof.
The Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: To proceed: We found people of different schools and creeds in religion, agreeing despite their differences on one word: that each one's own view is true and what others hold is false, by the consensus of their entire group, which has a predecessor they follow. So it is established that taqlid is not an excuse for its practitioner, because an opponent likewise has a similar (predecessor) against him, and there is nothing in it but numerical majority. Unless the one to whom the saying is referred is a proof whose actions show his truthfulness in what he claims, and a demonstration that compels the impartial to his correctness. So the one to whom recourse is made in religion by what necessitates verification from him is the truth-holder, and it is upon each of them to know the truth in what he professes, as if what he professes is [supported by] evidence and testimony of the truth for him – indeed it has attended them; for the end of each of their proofs is to submit to him if they were to obtain it, and it has appeared to whomever I mentioned, and it is not permissible that similar (proofs) appear to many in religion, because their proofs would contradict and show the concealment of causes of doubt in others. And there is no power except with God, the Almighty.
Source pages p. 602

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of taqlid (blind following) and a statement of the obligation to know religion by proof. The author, Imam Maturidi, begins by noting that people of different sects all claim their own view is true and others false, yet they follow earlier authorities blindly. If taqlid were valid, opposing groups would both be justified, leading to contradiction. Therefore, taqlid is not an acceptable excuse. The only exception is a person who possesses a proof of his truthfulness and a demonstration that compels others. In that case, he is the one truly correct. Each person must know the truth in his religion, as if he has direct evidence. The author then argues that if multiple groups had equal proofs, they would contradict each other, which is impossible. Thus, only one can have the true proof.

Key Terms
حجة
برهان
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is permissible to follow any scholar unconditionally.
Correction: Maturidi allows following only if the scholar himself has proof and demonstration, and even then, the follower should strive to know the truth.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible to follow someone in religious matters?
A) Always, if the person is known to be pious.
B) When the person is a proof (حجة) with actions showing truthfulness and a compelling demonstration.
C) Only when the person is a prophet.
D) Never, everyone must derive proofs independently.

Key Takeaway
Following a person is only valid when he has proof; each person has the duty to know the truth through evidence, and truth cannot be multiple.

The Two Sources of Religious Knowledge: Hearing and Intellect
Imam Maturidi identifies two fundamental sources of religion: hearing and intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi establishes hearing (revelation) and intellect as the two foundational sources for knowing religion.

Core Idea
Religion rests on two pillars: revelation and reason.

Imam Maturidi opens by stating that religion must have a foundation. He then identifies two fundamental sources: hearing (sam'), which refers to transmitted revelation and the reports of prophets, and intellect ('aql), which is the rational capacity to discern wisdom and purpose. This passage sets the stage for a balanced approach to Islamic theology, where both revelation and reason are indispensable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Notice how Imam Maturidi presents the two sources in a structured way.
2
Identify the two sources
The passage explicitly names 'hearing' (sam') and 'intellect' ('aql) as the two aspects of the foundation of religion. Highlight these terms and see how each is introduced.
These are not just sources but the very basis of religious knowledge.
3
Examine the argument for hearing
Imam Maturidi argues that hearing is universal: even skeptics adopt a creed and call others to it. He also points to the practice of kings and prophets as evidence. Note how he uses empirical observation to support his claim.
4
Examine the argument for intellect
The intellect argument is based on wisdom: the world cannot be created for mere annihilation, and it is unfitting for the Creator to act unwisely. Therefore, the world must have a purpose. Reflect on how this rational argument precedes revelation.
This shows that reason independently establishes the need for purpose.
5
Reflect on the relationship
Consider how both sources complement each other. Hearing provides the content of religion (the revealed message), while intellect confirms its necessity and wisdom. How does this balance prevent extremism in both scriptural literalism and rationalism?
This is a key methodological point in Maturidi theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أصل ما يعرف به الدين - إذ لا بد أن يكون لهذا الخالق دين يلزمهم الإجتماع عليه وأصل يلزمهم الفرع إليه وجهان: أحدهما السمع والآخر العقل. أما السمع فما لا يخلو بشر من انتحاله مذهبا يعتمد عليه ويدعو غيره إليه حتى شاركهم في ذلك أصحاب الشكوك والتجاهل فضلا عن الذي يقر بوجود الأشياء وتحقيقها. على ذلك جرت سياسة ملوك الأرض من سيرة كل تسوية أمورهم عليه وتأليف ما بين قلوب رعيتهم به، وكذلك أمر الذين ادعوا الرسالة والحكمة ومن قام بتدبير أنواع الصناعة، وبالله المعونة والنجاة - وأما العقل فهو أن كون هذا العالم للفناء خاصة ليس بحكمة، وخروج فعله كل ذي عقل عن طريق الحكمة قبيح عنه، فلا يحتمل أن يكون الذي العقل منه جزء مؤسسا على غير الحكمة أو مجعولا عبثا.
Then the fundamental source by which religion is known – since this Creator must have a religion requiring them to unite upon it and a foundation to which they must refer as branch – has two aspects: one is hearing (sam') and the other is intellect ('aql). As for hearing, it is that which no human being lacks in adopting a creed to rely upon and to call others to, even the people of doubts and ignorance participate in that, let alone those who affirm the existence and reality of things. Upon that has proceeded the policy of the kings of the earth, in the conduct of each settling their affairs and composing the hearts of their subjects, and likewise the affair of those who claimed prophethood and wisdom and those who undertook the management of various crafts. And with God is aid and deliverance. As for the intellect, it is that the existence of this world for annihilation alone is not wisdom, and the act of every intelligent being departing from the path of wisdom is ugly for him. So it is not possible that the one whose intellect is part (of him) is founded on other than wisdom or made in vain.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage classifies the two fundamental sources of religious knowledge: hearing (sam') and intellect ('aql). The author argues that there must be a religion from the Creator that unites people, and it has these two foundations. First, hearing (revelation or transmitted reports): every human being adopts some creed and calls others to it, even skeptics, showing that revelation is universal and indispensable. The author supports this by noting that earthly rulers use it to order their affairs and unite people, and prophets and wise men also rely on it. Second, intellect: the world is not created for mere annihilation, as that would be unwise. Every rational person considers departing from wisdom as ugly. Therefore, it is inconceivable that the One who gave intellect (God) would create the world without purpose or wisdom. The intellect itself testifies to the necessity of purpose and wisdom in creation.

Key Terms
سمع (hearing/revelation)
عقل (intellect)
أصل (foundation/source)
حكمة (wisdom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that religion is based solely on revelation (sam') and that reason plays no independent role.
Correction: Imam Maturidi explicitly includes intellect as a fundamental source, arguing that reason independently establishes the wisdom and purpose of creation, which complements revelation.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Maturidi, what does the intellect ('aql) establish about the creation of the world?
A) That the world was created for mere annihilation.
B) That the world cannot be created in vain or without wisdom.
C) That the world is eternal.
D) That the world was created by chance.

Key Takeaway
Imam Maturidi establishes that both transmitted revelation (sam') and rational intellect ('aql) are essential and complementary sources for understanding religion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to follow revelation (sam'), but have you truly used your intellect ('aql) to question whether your life is aligned with the wisdom of your Creator? What specific step will you take today to reflect on the purpose behind one difficulty you face?
?
The passage says no one lacks a creed. What creed are you actually living by— through your daily choices— that might contradict the truth of both hearing and intellect? Who are you calling to that creed through your actions?
?
If the world is not for annihilation alone, then your existence has a purpose beyond this fleeting life. What comforts or pursuits are you clinging to as if you will stay here forever? Will you let that go starting today?

The Two Foundations of Religion: Revelation and Intellect
Maturidi defines and argues for the two sources of religious knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi identifies revelation (sam‘) and intellect (‘aql) as the two essential foundations for knowing religion, and how revelation is universally relied upon.

Core Idea
Revelation and intellect together form the basis of all religious knowledge.

Maturidi begins his theological treatise by establishing the two sources from which all religious knowledge is derived. He argues that both revelation and intellect are necessary, and then shows how revelation is universally acknowledged even by skeptics and rulers. This passage sets the stage for the entire discipline of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the flow from definition to argumentation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two foundations
Find where Maturidi explicitly names revelation and intellect as the two aspects of the foundation of religion.
They are introduced right after 'two aspects'.
3
Understand the argument for revelation
Maturidi argues that no human is free from adopting a doctrine (madhhab). Notice how he includes even skeptics and kings to show the universality of revelation.
This counters the idea that reason alone suffices.
4
Reflect on the final prayer
The passage ends with 'And with God is assistance and deliverance.' Consider why Maturidi ends this foundational statement with a prayer.
It shows reliance on divine help even in intellectual matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السمع والعقل هما أصل ما يعرف به الدين. ثم أصل ما يعرف به الدين إذ لا بد أن يكون لهذا الخالق دين يلزمهم الاجتماع عليه وأصل يلزمهم الفرع إليه وجهان: أحدهما السمع والآخر العقل. أما السمع فما لا يخلو بشر من انتحال مذهب يعتمد عليه ويدعو غيره إليه حتى شاركهم في ذلك أصحاب الشكوك والتجاهل فضلا عن الذي يقر بوجود الأشياء وتحقيقها. على ذلك جرت سياسة ملوك الأرض من سيرة كل تسوية أمورهم عليه وتأليف ما بين قلوب رعيتهم به، وكذلك أمر الذين ادعوا الرسالة والحكمة ومن قام بتدبير أنواع الصناعة. وبالله المعونة والنجاة.
Revelation and intellect are the two foundations by which religion is known. Then the foundation of what is known of religion—for it is necessary that this Creator has a religion which obliges them to unite upon, and a foundation to which they must refer—has two aspects: one is revelation, and the other is intellect. As for revelation, there is no human being free from adopting a doctrine (madhhab) that he relies upon and calls others to, so much so that the people of doubts and ignorance share in that, let alone those who affirm the existence of things and their reality. Upon this the politics of the kings of the earth proceeded, each one settling their affairs and reconciling the hearts of their subjects through it, as well as those who claimed prophethood and wisdom, and those who managed various crafts. And with God is assistance and deliverance.
Source pages p. 603

Commentary
This passage defines the two foundations of religious knowledge. Maturidi begins by stating that revelation (sam‘) and intellect (‘aql) are the two origins by which religion is known. He then explains that there must be a religion from the Creator that obliges people to unite upon it, and that the foundation to which everything else refers has two aspects: revelation and intellect. For revelation, he argues that no human is free from adopting a doctrine (madhhab) that he relies upon and propagates, even skeptics and the ignorant. This shows the universality of revelation. He supports this by noting that kings govern by it to settle affairs and reconcile hearts, and prophets and craftsmen also rely on it. The passage ends with a prayer for God's assistance.

Two Faulty Methods: Inspiration and Incapacity
Maturidi presents two opinions on the means of knowing truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two flawed epistemological methods that people have proposed for knowing truth and benefit.

Core Idea
Truth cannot be known by mere inner feelings or human incapacity.

Maturidi begins by reporting the views of those who claim that truth is known by what falls into the heart, or by acknowledging human incapacity. These two opinions, though widely held, are rejected by sound reasoning. This lesson presents these views before Maturidi's critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Observe the two contrasting opinions presented.
Notice the phrase 'ما يقع في قلب' and 'يعجز البشر'.
2
Identify the two methods
Identify the first method: knowledge through what falls into the heart (inner inspiration). Identify the second: acknowledging human incapacity and clinging to what is given.
These are two distinct epistemological claims.
3
Reflect on their basis
Consider why someone might adopt each view. The first relies on subjective certainty; the second on humility before divine management.
Maturidi will later show both are insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم اختلف في الأسباب التي بها يُعلم المصالح والحق والمحاسن من أضدادها فمنهم من يقول : هو ما يقع في قلب كل منهم حُسنه لزمه التمسك به ، ومنهم من يقول : يعجز البشر عن الإحاطة بالسبب ولكن يتمسك بما أنهم ؛ لما يكون ذلك ممن له تدبير العالم . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهما بعيدان من أن يكونا من أسباب المعرفة ؛ لأن وجوه التضاد والتناقض في الأديان بين ، ثم عند كل واحد أنه المحق ، ومحال أن يكون سبب الحق يعمل هذا العمل ، لما تصور الباطل بنفس صورة الحق ، فمحال الثقة بمن ظهر كذبه كل هذا الظهور ما كان معتقدا لمذهب باعتقاد الحق بما ذكرت عند ضده ، وفى إلهامه أنه مبطل، ولم يكن لواحد منهما دليل غير الذي لآخر في خطابه، وذلك نوع ما لا يدفع الاختلاف والتضاد اللذين بهما التفاني . وعلى ذلك / يبطل اعلام القرعة فيما يعجز عنه ذو العقل ، ولم يجعل في الحكم الجبر على الرضا إذ هي تخرج مختلفا ، وكذلك أمر القائف يجز أن يكونا سيبى الحق . ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then they differed regarding the means by which benefits, truth, and goods are known from their opposites. Some of them say: It is what falls into the heart of each of them—its goodness necessitates adherence to it. Some of them say: Humans are incapable of encompassing the cause, but they cling to what they are given, since that is from the one who manages the world. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: Both are far from being means of knowledge, because aspects of opposition and contradiction in religions are clear. Then each one believes that he is the truth-holder, and it is impossible that the cause of truth works this way—because falsehood assumes the same form as truth. So it is impossible to trust one whose falsehood has appeared so clearly while he is a follower of a doctrine, believing it is truth, while what I mentioned is against its opposite, and in his inspiration that he is false. And neither of them has any proof other than what the other has in his statement, and that is a type that does not prevent the difference and opposition that lead to annihilation. Accordingly, the indication of casting lots is nullified in what a rational person is incapable of, and compulsion to satisfaction is not made in judgment since it emerges differently, and likewise the matter of the qa'if (tracker) suffices that they might be the two causes of truth. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two flawed epistemological methods for determining truth and benefit. Maturidi first reports two opinions: (1) That truth is known by what falls into the heart of each person—if it seems good, one must adhere to it. (2) That humans are incapable of grasping the cause, so they simply cling to what they are given by the one who manages the world. Maturidi declares both views far from being valid means of knowledge. His criticism is based on the clear opposition and contradiction among religions, where each party claims to hold the truth. Since falsehood can appear in the same form as truth, it is impossible to trust anyone whose apparent falsity is evident, especially when they sincerely believe their own doctrine. Moreover, neither side possesses a proof that is unique to them; each relies on the same type of subjective inspiration or incapacity, which does not resolve conflict. He extends this critique to the practice of casting lots (القرعة) in matters beyond human reason, and to the idea of compulsion in judgment (الجبر على الرضا), since these can produce differing results. He also mentions the qa'if (a tracker of footprints), implying that such methods are insufficient for establishing truth. The passage ends with a prayer for divine aid.

Key Terms
أسباب المعرفة
الإلهام
المصالح
الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the first group claim is the means of knowing truth?
A) Rational deduction
B) What falls into the heart
C) Consensus of scholars
D) Divine revelation

Key Takeaway
Maturidi reports two erroneous methods—inner inspiration and human incapacity—before refuting them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel strongly that something is right, do you ever pause to verify that feeling against revealed knowledge, or do you assume the feeling itself is enough? What is at stake if you choose wrongly?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you tell yourself 'I can never understand' and stop learning? How does that excuse affect your relationship with the Truth?

Maturidi's Refutation: Contradiction and Impossibility
Maturidi refutes the two methods using the argument of contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that subjective inspiration and appeals to incapacity fail because they cannot resolve contradiction and falsehood can mimic truth.

Core Idea
If falsehood can appear as truth, subjective inspiration cannot be trusted.

Maturidi now delivers his refutation of the two methods. He points out the evident contradiction among religions and the fact that each side claims truth. Since falsehood can assume the same form as truth, no one can rely on mere inspiration. He extends this critique to practices like casting lots and compulsion in judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the logical steps Maturidi uses to critique the two methods.
The passage starts with 'قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهما بعيدان...'
2
Identify the key argument
Identify that Maturidi uses the existence of contradiction and the fact that falsehood can resemble truth as his main points.
Notice the phrase 'لما تصور الباطل بنفس صورة الحق'.
3
Understand the impossibility of trust
Because each claimant has the same type of evidence (subjective feeling), and contradiction is unresolved, neither can be trusted.
The result is that these methods lead to 'التفاني' (annihilation).
4
See the extension to other practices
Maturidi also rejects casting lots (القرعة) and compulsion in judgment (الجبر على الرضا) on similar grounds.
These practices also fail to provide consistent, objective truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم اختلف في الأسباب التي بها يُعلم المصالح والحق والمحاسن من أضدادها فمنهم من يقول : هو ما يقع في قلب كل منهم حُسنه لزمه التمسك به ، ومنهم من يقول : يعجز البشر عن الإحاطة بالسبب ولكن يتمسك بما أنهم ؛ لما يكون ذلك ممن له تدبير العالم . قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهما بعيدان من أن يكونا من أسباب المعرفة ؛ لأن وجوه التضاد والتناقض في الأديان بين ، ثم عند كل واحد أنه المحق ، ومحال أن يكون سبب الحق يعمل هذا العمل ، لما تصور الباطل بنفس صورة الحق ، فمحال الثقة بمن ظهر كذبه كل هذا الظهور ما كان معتقدا لمذهب باعتقاد الحق بما ذكرت عند ضده ، وفى إلهامه أنه مبطل، ولم يكن لواحد منهما دليل غير الذي لآخر في خطابه، وذلك نوع ما لا يدفع الاختلاف والتضاد اللذين بهما التفاني . وعلى ذلك / يبطل اعلام القرعة فيما يعجز عنه ذو العقل ، ولم يجعل في الحكم الجبر على الرضا إذ هي تخرج مختلفا ، وكذلك أمر القائف يجز أن يكونا سيبى الحق . ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: Then they differed regarding the means by which benefits, truth, and goods are known from their opposites. Some of them say: It is what falls into the heart of each of them—its goodness necessitates adherence to it. Some of them say: Humans are incapable of encompassing the cause, but they cling to what they are given, since that is from the one who manages the world. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: Both are far from being means of knowledge, because aspects of opposition and contradiction in religions are clear. Then each one believes that he is the truth-holder, and it is impossible that the cause of truth works this way—because falsehood assumes the same form as truth. So it is impossible to trust one whose falsehood has appeared so clearly while he is a follower of a doctrine, believing it is truth, while what I mentioned is against its opposite, and in his inspiration that he is false. And neither of them has any proof other than what the other has in his statement, and that is a type that does not prevent the difference and opposition that lead to annihilation. Accordingly, the indication of casting lots is nullified in what a rational person is incapable of, and compulsion to satisfaction is not made in judgment since it emerges differently, and likewise the matter of the qa'if (tracker) suffices that they might be the two causes of truth. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two flawed epistemological methods for determining truth and benefit. Maturidi first reports two opinions: (1) That truth is known by what falls into the heart of each person—if it seems good, one must adhere to it. (2) That humans are incapable of grasping the cause, so they simply cling to what they are given by the one who manages the world. Maturidi declares both views far from being valid means of knowledge. His criticism is based on the clear opposition and contradiction among religions, where each party claims to hold the truth. Since falsehood can appear in the same form as truth, it is impossible to trust anyone whose apparent falsity is evident, especially when they sincerely believe their own doctrine. Moreover, neither side possesses a proof that is unique to them; each relies on the same type of subjective inspiration or incapacity, which does not resolve conflict. He extends this critique to the practice of casting lots (القرعة) in matters beyond human reason, and to the idea of compulsion in judgment (الجبر على الرضا), since these can produce differing results. He also mentions the qa'if (a tracker of footprints), implying that such methods are insufficient for establishing truth. The passage ends with a prayer for divine aid.

Key Terms
التضاد
التناقض
القرعة
الجبر
القائف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That inner inspiration or human incapacity can reliably determine truth and resolve disagreements.
Correction: Maturidi argues that because falsehood can assume the same form as truth and contradictions remain, these methods cannot be trusted and lead to destruction.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is it impossible to trust someone based on their inner inspiration?
A) Because all humans are liars
B) Because falsehood can appear in the same form as truth
C) Because inspiration only comes from God
D) Because the heart is weak

Key Takeaway
Subjective inspiration and appeals to incapacity fail because they cannot resolve contradiction and falsehood can mimic truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What will you do the next time you feel a strong inner conviction that contradicts clear evidence?
?
Think of a deeply held belief you have about your faith or life that you have never questioned because it 'feels true.' If falsehood can appear exactly like truth, on what objective grounds do you know that belief is actually true? Are you willing to examine that belief today?
?
Maturidi says that relying on subjective inspiration leads to mutual annihilation because it cannot resolve differences. Where in your relationships or community do you see people holding opposing views each claiming divine guidance? How has your own insistence on your 'inspiration' contributed to division rather than unity? What concrete step can you take this week to seek objective proof before acting on a feeling?

Why Subjective Feeling and Blind Authority Fail as Sources of Knowledge
Maturidi presents and refutes two false epistemological theories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi rejects both subjective intuition and blind conformity to authority as reliable methods for distinguishing truth from falsehood.

Core Idea
Subjective feeling and blind authority both fail because falsehood can perfectly imitate truth.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a fundamental question: how can humans reliably know what is true and beneficial? He examines two commonly proposed but flawed answers—trusting one's inner feeling, and blindly following authoritative commands. By exposing the inevitable contradictions that arise from both, he demonstrates the need for an objective criterion grounded in evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation without analysis. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فمحال الثقة بمن ظهر كذبه'.
2
Identify the two false views
Highlight the descriptions of the two views: one based on subjective feeling (ما يقع في قلب كل منهم) and the other on blind obedience (يتمسك بما أنهم). Notice how they are presented as opposites.
Both views are introduced with 'فمنهم من يقول'.
3
Analyze the refutation
Study the shaykh's argument: because falsehood can appear as truth, and each side claims to be right, neither subjective nor authoritarian methods can resolve contradictions. Reflect on the examples of lot-casting and the tracker (al-qa'if).
The passage concludes that these methods lead to mutual destruction (التفاني).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم اختلف في الأسباب التي بها يُعلم المصالح والحق والمحاسن من أضدادها فمنهم من يقول : هو ما يقع في قلب كل منهم حُسنه لزمه التمسك به ، ومنهم من يقول : يعجز البشر عن الإحاطة بالسبب ولكن يتمسك بما أنهم ؛ لما يكون ذلك ممن له تدبير العالم .
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : وهما بعيدان من أن يكونا من أسباب المعرفة ؛ لأن وجوه التضاد والتناقض في الأديان بين ، ثم عند كل واحد أنه المحق ، ومحال أن يكون سبب الحق يعمل هذا العمل ، لما تصور الباطل بنفس صورة الحق ، فمحال الثقة بمن ظهر كذبه كل هذا الظهور ما كان معتقدا لمذهب باعتقاد الحق بما ذكرت عند ضده ، وفى إلهامه أنه مبطل، ولم يكن لواحد منهما دليل غير الذي لآخر في خطابه، وذلك نوع ما لا يدفع الاختلاف والتضاد اللذين بهما التفاني . وعلى ذلك / يبطل اعلام القرعة فيما يعجز عنه ذو العقل ، ولم يجعل في الحكم الجبر على الرضا إذ هي تخرج مختلفا ، وكذلك أمر القائف يجز أن يكونا سيبى الحق . ولا قوة إلا بالله
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then they differed concerning the causes by which beneficial things, truth, and good qualities are known from their opposites. Some of them say: It is what falls into the heart of each of them (as) its goodness, then he must adhere to it. Some of them say: Humans are incapable of encompassing the cause, but they adhere to what they have been commanded, because that is from the one who has the governance of the world. The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Both are far from being among the causes of knowledge, because the aspects of opposition and contradiction in religions are manifest, and then each one believes that he is right. It is impossible that the cause of truth should work this work, since falsehood assumes the very form of truth. So it is impossible to trust one whose falsehood has appeared in all this appearance, while he believed in a doctrine by believing the truth as I mentioned against his opposite, and in his inspiration that he is false. Neither had any proof other than that of the other in his speech. That is a type that does not repel the difference and contradiction which entail mutual destruction. And accordingly, the announcement of the lot casting is invalidated in what the intelligent person is incapable of, and he did not make in the judgment compulsion to satisfaction, since it comes out differing. Likewise, the matter of the tracker (al-qa'if) is such that they would be the two causes of truth. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 605

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes two false views about how humans can know what is beneficial (masalih) and true (haqq) as opposed to their opposites. The first view is subjectivism: whatever feels good to one's heart is true and must be followed. The second view is a kind of authoritarianism: humans cannot grasp the cause themselves, so they must blindly follow authoritative commands from the one who governs the world (likely referring to rulers or religious leaders). The Shaykh (commentator) rejects both. He argues that religions are full of contradictions, and each side claims truth. If truth were based on subjective feeling or authority, falsehood could easily disguise itself as truth. Therefore, no one can be trusted if they have been shown to be false, even if they claim inspiration. Both parties lack evidence for their claim except their own statements, which leads to irreconcilable differences and mutual destruction. He also invalidates the use of lot-casting (qur'a) for matters beyond human reason, and notes that there is no compulsion in judgment to accept satisfaction. The reference to 'al-qa'if' (a tracker of footprints) illustrates that even experts can produce conflicting 'causes' of truth. The overall conclusion is that these two methods cannot be reliable paths to objective knowledge.

Key Terms
الأسباب
المصالح
الحق
المحاسن
التضاد
التناقض
القرعة
القائف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that intuition or deference to authority is sufficient to know truth.
Correction: Maturidi shows that both are unreliable because falsehood can imitate truth, and contradictory claims arise without objective criteria.

Checkpoint

A) They are forbidden in Islam.
B) They lead to contradiction because falsehood can appear in the same form as truth.
C) They are too difficult for humans to use.
D) They are only valid for animals.

Key Takeaway
Neither inner feeling nor blind obedience can be trusted as sources of truth because falsehood can disguise itself as truth, leading to unresolvable contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Three Sources of Knowledge and the Definition of Sensory Perception
Maturidi lists three ways to knowledge and defines sensory perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi identifies three ways to attain knowledge of realities, with sensory perception as the foundational source.

Core Idea
Sensory perception is the bedrock of certain knowledge.

Maturidi begins his epistemology by listing three pathways to knowledge: sensory perception, reports, and reasoning. He then defines the first, sensory perception, as the foundation of knowledge that has no opposite. This lesson clarifies that sensory knowledge is necessary and undeniable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three sources
Read the Arabic passage once. Note that Maturidi lists three ways to attain knowledge of realities: sensory perception, reports, and reasoning. See how he introduces them with 'thumma al-sabeel'.
The three sources are 'al-‘iyan, al-akhbar, al-nazar'.
2
Focus on the definition of 'iyan
Maturidi defines sensory perception as 'what falls under the senses'. He declares it the foundation of a kind of knowledge that has no opposite from ignorance.
This knowledge is necessary (daruri) and self-evident.
3
Reflect on why sensory perception is foundational
Consider why Maturidi calls it 'the foundation'. The next lesson will show that denying it is self-contradictory, but here we learn that it is the basis for all other knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم السبيل التى يوصل بها إلى العلم بحقائق الأشياء العيان والأخبار والنظر . فالعيان ما يقع عليه الحواس ، وهو الأصل الذي لديه العلم الذى لا ضد له من الجهل. فمن قال بضده من الجهل فهو الذي يسمى منكرة كل سامع مكابرا تأبى طبيعة البهائم أن يكون ذلك رتبتها ؛ إذ كل منها يعلم ما به بقاؤها وفناؤها وما يتلذذ به ويتألم ، وصاحب هذا ينكر ذلك ، وأجمع أن لا يناظر مع من كان ذلك قوله ؛ إذ لا يثبت إنكاره ولا حضوره ، بنفسه ، والمناظرة في مائية الشيء أو هستيته وهو لها، وللدفع جميعا دافع ولكنه يمازج فيقال له : تعلم بأنك تنفى ، فإن قال : لا ، بطل نفيه ، وإن أثبت نفيه ، فيصير بما يدفع دافعا لدفعه . ويؤلم . ويؤلم بالألم الشديد من قال : نعم قطع الجوارح ليدع تعنته ، إذ نحن نعلم أنه يعلم العيان ؛ إذ هو علم الضرورة ، ولكنه بقوله متعنتا ، وحق مثله ما ذكرتُ ليجزع ويضجر فيقابل بتعنت فينهتك لديه ستره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the ways by which knowledge of the realities of things is attained are sensory perception, reports, and reasoning. Sensory perception is what falls under the senses, and it is the foundation upon which rests the knowledge that has no opposite from ignorance. Whoever says the opposite from ignorance is one who is called a denier, every hearer (calls him) an obstinate one, for the nature of beasts refuses that this should be their rank—since each of them knows what is its survival and its destruction, and what it delights in and what it suffers. The one who holds this view denies that. It is agreed that one should not debate with one who holds such a statement, because his denial is not established, nor his presence of mind, by himself. Discussion about the essence (ma'iyya) of a thing or its existence (hastiyya) while he is for them (i.e., the things), and for the repulsion (of denial) there is a repeller (i.e., the senses), but he mixes (things). So it is said to him: Do you know that you deny? If he says: No, his denial becomes null. If he affirms his denial, then by what he repels he becomes a repeller of his own repelling. And he is pained. He is pained with severe pain if he says: Yes, cut off the limbs to leave his obstinacy, for we know that he knows sensory perception because it is necessary knowledge. But by his statement he is obstinate. The treatment for someone like him is what I have mentioned to make him distressed and annoyed, so let him be met with obstinacy until his covering is torn away. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of sensory perception ('iyan) as one of the three ways to attain knowledge of realities. Maturidi begins by listing the three ways: sensory perception, reports, and reasoning. He then defines 'iyan as what is grasped by the senses and declares it the foundation of a kind of knowledge that has no opposite (i.e., ignorance). He argues that anyone who denies sensory knowledge is utterly obstinate, because even animals possess it—they know what preserves them and what harms them. Such a denier cannot be rationally debated, because his very denial is self-contradictory. If you ask him whether he knows that he denies, he either says 'no' (nullifying his denial) or 'yes' (affirming his denial, which itself is a form of knowledge). The denier is likened to one who claims he does not feel pain even when limbs are cut, which is clearly false. The passage emphasizes that sensory knowledge is necessary (daruri) and undeniable; anyone who rejects it is merely being obstinate (muta'annit) and deserves to be confounded until his pretense is exposed.

Key Terms
العيان
الأخبار
النظر
الحواس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what are the three ways to knowledge?
A) Revelation, reason, senses
B) Sensory perception, reports, reasoning
C) Intuition, deduction, observation
D) Scripture, consensus, analogy

Key Takeaway
Sensory perception is the first and foundational source of certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever doubted your own senses when they told you something about the oneness of Allah? When you see the order of creation, do you treat that sensory evidence as certain knowledge, or do you allow doubts to creep in? Commit today to treat the clear signs of Allah as undeniable and act upon them.

?
The passage says sensory perception is the foundation of knowledge that has no opposite from ignorance. Is your faith built on that certain foundation, or is it built on shaky assumptions? What step can you take today to reinforce your certainty in the truths you perceive?

Refuting the Denier of Sensory Knowledge
Maturidi refutes the denier of sensory perception with a self-refutation argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that denying sensory perception is self-defeating and how to respond to such denial.

Core Idea
To deny sensory perception is to deny the very ability to deny.

Having established sensory perception as a foundation of knowledge, Maturidi now addresses the person who would deny it. He argues that such denial is inherently contradictory and even animals possess sensory knowledge. This lesson walks through the dialectical exchange that exposes the denier's absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the denier and the initial response
Read the passage and find the description of the person who denies sensory perception. Notice that such a person is called a 'denier' (munkir) and 'obstinate' (mukabir). Maturidi says it is agreed not to debate him because his denial is not established.
The passage uses strong language: 'even beasts refuse this station'.
2
Analyze the dialectical argument
Focus on the exchange where the denier is asked: 'Do you know that you deny?' If he says no, his denial is null. If he says yes, then he affirms his denial, which itself is an act of knowledge, contradicting his position.
This is a classic self-refutation argument.
3
Consider the cutting of limbs example
Maturidi gives an extreme example: if the denier claims not to feel pain when his limbs are cut, his obstinacy is clear. The pain itself is sensory knowledge that he cannot deny.
The purpose is to show that sensory knowledge is necessary (daruri) and undeniable.
4
Understand the prescribed treatment
The passage concludes that such a person should be met with obstinacy to tear away his pretense. Reflect on why Maturidi considers this appropriate.
The denier is not genuinely rational; he is being willfully obstinate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Then the ways by which knowledge of the realities of things is attained are sensory perception, reports, and reasoning. Sensory perception is what falls under the senses, and it is the foundation upon which rests the knowledge that has no opposite from ignorance. So it is said to him: Do you know that you deny? If he says: No, his denial becomes null. If he affirms his denial, then by what he repels he becomes a repeller of his own repelling. And he is pained. He is pained with severe pain if he says: Yes, cut off the limbs to leave his obstinacy, for we know that he knows sensory perception because it is necessary knowledge. He then defines 'iyan as what is grasped by the senses and declares it the foundation of a kind of knowledge that has no opposite (i.e., ignorance). ?

The Two Types of Reports and the Self-Contradiction of Denial
Maturidi classifies reports and critiques their denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of reports into two types and why denying reports leads to self-contradiction and the loss of foundational knowledge.

Core Idea
Denying reports is self-defeating because the denial itself is a report.

Maturidi begins by distinguishing two kinds of reports, then immediately turns to the problem of denying reports altogether. He argues that anyone who rejects the validity of reports falls into a contradiction: his denial is itself a report. Moreover, such denial undermines all knowledge that depends on testimony, including knowledge of one's own identity and daily necessities. This passage establishes the foundational role of reports in human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the density of Maturidi's style.
The text is slightly cut off at the end, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the two types of reports
Maturidi states that reports are of two types, though he does not enumerate them here. Reflect on what these two types might be (e.g., mutawatir and ahad, or reports from the senses vs. reports from testimony).
The classification is not detailed in this excerpt, but it sets the stage for the argument against denial.
3
Understand the self-contradiction argument
Maturidi points out that the denier's own denial is a report. Thus, by denying reports, he denies his own denial—a logical inconsistency. Discuss why this refutes the position of radical skepticism about reports.
4
Reflect on the consequences of denial
The passage lists consequences: ignorance of lineage, name, essence, and even practical knowledge like livelihood. Consider how rejecting reports would make everyday life impossible.
This shows that reports are indispensable for basic human knowledge.
5
Connect to theological methodology
Recognize that this argument supports the reliability of transmitted knowledge (sam'iyyat) in Islamic theology. If reports were unreliable, revelation itself would be undermined.
Maturidi is defending the epistemological foundation for receiving divine messages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأخبار نوعان ، من أذكر جملته لحق بالفريق الأول ؛ لأنه أنكر إنكاره ؛ إذ إنكاره خبر ، فيصير منكرا عند إنكاره -- إنكاره ، ما فيه جهل نسبه واسمه ومائيته واسم جوهره واسم كل شيء ، فيجب به جهل أن يُخبر عن شي. عاينه إذا خبر به ، فكيف يبلغ هو إلى العلم بما يبلغه مما غاب عنه ، أو متى يعلم ما به معاشه / وغذاؤه ، وكل ذلك يصل
The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Reports are of two types. Whoever mentions its summary joins the first group, because he denies his denial, since his denial is a report. So he becomes a denier when his denial [is considered]. His denial contains ignorance of his lineage, his name, his essence, the name of his substance, and the name of everything. So it necessitates ignorance that he should report about something he has perceived when he reports about it. How can he attain knowledge of what he conveys from what is absent from him? Or when can he know that by which his livelihood and nourishment [are obtained]? And all that reaches [him].
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage, though somewhat cryptic, presents a classification of reports (akhbar) into two types and then argues against those who deny the validity of reports as a source of knowledge. The Shaykh begins by stating that reports are two kinds. He then points out that anyone who denies the reliability of reports is effectively denying his own denial, because the denial itself is a report. Therefore, such a person becomes a denier of his own statement. The denial of reports leads to ignorance about basic matters such as lineage, names, and essences, because all such knowledge comes through reports. If a person rejects reports, he cannot even trust that someone is reporting about something they have witnessed. Moreover, he cannot attain knowledge of things absent from him, nor can he know his own means of livelihood, which are often learned through reports. The passage emphasizes that all these necessities depend on the validity of reports, and denying them renders knowledge impossible. The text cuts off before completing the argument.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying the reliability of reports (akhbar) is a harmless form of skepticism.
Correction: Maturidi shows that denying reports leads to a self-contradiction and to the impossibility of knowing even one's own identity and basic needs, making skepticism unsustainable.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what consequence follows if a person denies the validity of reports?
A) He becomes a disbeliever.
B) He loses knowledge of his own lineage, name, and livelihood.
C) He must rely solely on reason.
D) He gains certainty through intuition.

Key Takeaway
Denying reports is self-contradictory and leads to ignorance, thus reports are essential for knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reject a report about your own shortcomings from someone else, do you see that your rejection itself is a claim that could be examined? How does this lesson challenge you to accept uncomfortable reports about yourself?
?
Think of a single belief you hold about your identity—your lineage, your name, or your character. Trace it back: did you learn it entirely through your own experience, or through the testimony of others? If you deny reports, what happens to that belief?
?
You rely on reports for your daily bread—food labels, market prices, cooking instructions. If you cannot trust reports, how can you trust the divine reports that guide your spiritual sustenance? What would it take for you to accept the reports of revelation with the same certainty you have for mundane reports?

The Three Paths to Knowledge: Sensory Perception as Foundation
Maturidi enumerates the three ways to knowledge and defines empirical perception as foundational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three paths to certain knowledge in Islamic theology, with emphasis on sensory perception as the foundational source.

Core Idea
Sensory knowledge is undeniable—even beasts possess it.

Maturidi begins his epistemological framework by outlining the three valid paths to knowledge: sensory perception, reliable reports, and rational inference. He anchors the discussion on sensory experience, demonstrating that it provides necessary knowledge that every creature, including animals, instinctively relies upon. This sets the stage for refuting those who deny the reality of sensory information.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining the three paths
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the first path: al-'iyān
Find the definition of empirical perception (al-'iyān) and note how Maturidi describes it as what the senses fall upon.
3
Understand why al-'iyān is foundational
Maturidi states that al-'iyān is the foundation because it provides necessary knowledge (ḍarūrah) that has no opposite in ignorance. This means it is self-evident and cannot be rationally doubted.
4
Reflect on the animal analogy
Why does Maturidi mention beasts? He argues that even animals possess sensory knowledge, proving its universality and necessity.
This emphasizes that denying sensory knowledge is unnatural.
5
Connect to the other two paths
Consider how reports and reasoning depend on sensory experience. For example, reports rely on hearing (a sense), and reasoning often uses sensory data.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السبل الموصلة إلى العلم هي العيان والأخبار والنظر

قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم السبيل التى يوصل بها إلى العلم بحقائق الأشياء العيان والأخبار والنظر . فالعيان ما يقع عليه الحواس ، وهو الأصل الذي لديه العلم الذى لا ضد له من الجهل. فمن قال بضده من الجهل فهو الذي يسمى منكرة كل سامع مكابرا تأبى طبيعة البهائم أن يكون ذلك رتبتها ؛ إذ كل منها يعلم ما به بقاؤها وفناؤها وما يتلذذ به ويتألم ، وصاحب هذا ينكر ذلك ، وأجمع أن لا يناظر مع من كان ذلك قوله ؛ إذ لا يثبت إنكاره ولا حضوره ، بنفسه ، والمناظرة في مائية الشيء أو هستيته وهو لها، وللدفع جميعا دافع ولكنه يمازج فيقال له : تعلم بأنك تنفى ، فإن قال : لا ، بطل نفيه ، وإن أثبت نفيه ، فيصير بما يدفع دافعا لدفعه . ويؤلم . ويؤلم بالألم الشديد من قال : نعم قطع الجوارح ليدع تعنته ، إذ نحن نعلم أنه يعلم العيان ؛ إذ هو علم الضرورة ، ولكنه بقوله متعنتا ، وحق مثله ما ذكرتُ ليجزع ويضجر فيقابل بتعنت فينهتك لديه ستره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
The paths leading to knowledge are empirical perception (al-'iyān), reports (al-akhbār), and reasoning (al-naẓar). Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The path by which one arrives at knowledge of the realities of things is empirical perception, reports, and reasoning. Empirical perception is what the senses fall upon, and it is the foundation which possesses knowledge that has no opposite in ignorance. Whoever says its opposite from ignorance is called a denier, a stubborn disputant whom the nature of beasts refuses to have such a rank; for each of them knows what constitutes its survival and perishing, and what it finds pleasurable and painful. Yet this person denies that. It is agreed that one does not debate with someone who holds such a statement, for his denial is not established, nor his presence in himself. Debate is about the quiddity (mā'iyyah) or existence (hastiyyah) of a thing, and he is for it. For every push there is a pusher, but he mixes things up. So it is said to him: 'Do you know that you are denying?' If he says 'No,' his denial is invalidated. If he affirms his denial, then by what he pushes he becomes a pusher of his own push. He is pained, and pained with severe pain. Whoever says 'Yes' cuts off his limbs to abandon his obstinacy, for we know that he knows empirical perception because it is necessary knowledge (ḍarūrah). But he is obstinate in his statement. The treatment of such a person is what I mentioned, so that he may be frightened and annoyed, and be met with obstinacy so that his cover is torn. There is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage defines the three established paths to certain knowledge in Islamic theology: empirical perception (al-'iyān), which includes all sensory experience; reports (al-akhbār), which are testimonies; and reasoning (al-naẓar), which is intellectual inference. The author focuses first on empirical perception as the foundation, since it gives knowledge that is necessary and undeniable—even animals possess it. He then describes how to refute someone who denies the validity of sensory knowledge. Such a person is called a 'denier' and a 'stubborn disputant'. The refutation method is to ask whether he knows that he is denying. If he says no, his denial is meaningless. If he says yes, then he concedes that he has some knowledge, and by pushing his denial he actually pushes against himself. The author even suggests that if the denier persists, one may apply severe pain, for even beasts know pain, and the denier's own knowledge of pain proves his acceptance of sensory knowledge. The goal is to expose his obstinacy and force him to retract. This passage establishes the epistemological role of sense perception as the basis for all further knowledge in kalam.

Key Terms
العيان
الأخبار
النظر
الحواس
الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'al-'iyān' refers only to sight, but it includes all senses.
Correction: Maturidi says 'what the senses fall upon' (al-ḥawāss), which includes sight, hearing, touch, taste, and smell.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the foundational path to knowledge?
A) Reports (al-akhbār)
B) Empirical perception (al-'iyān)
C) Reasoning (al-naẓar)
D) Intuition

Key Takeaway
The first and most certain path to knowledge is sensory perception, which provides necessary knowledge shared by all creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi says empirical perception has no opposite in ignorance. When was the last time you truly trusted your sensory experience of God's creation as undeniable proof of His existence, rather than overthinking it?
?
Even animals possess sensory knowledge. You have been given intellect to go beyond the senses. Are you using your intellect to confirm the truth your senses already testify to, or are you stuck in doubts that ignore the obvious?
?
You claim to believe that sensory knowledge is certain, yet you neglect to reflect on the physical signs around you. Look at one tangible sign of God today with the conviction that it is a certain proof. Will you do that tomorrow?

Refuting the Denier of Sensory Knowledge
Maturidi refutes the denier of sensory perception through logical paradox and appeal to necessary knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method Maturidi prescribes for refuting someone who denies the validity of sensory perception.

Core Idea
Even the denier of sense knowledge proves its reality by his own denial.

Having established sensory perception as the foundation of knowledge, Maturidi now addresses the skeptic who denies it entirely. He provides a logical and even physical refutation, showing that the denier's own position is self-contradictory. The passage outlines a method to expose such stubborn denial and compel the opponent to acknowledge the inevitability of sensory knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the tone and the structure of the argument.
Maturidi uses a rhetorical question and an extreme hypothetical.
2
Identify the type of opponent
Maturidi calls the denier a 'stubborn disputant' (mukābir) and says even beasts are better. Understand the severity of this accusation.
3
Understand the logical trap
The key question: 'Do you know that you are denying?' If the denier says no, his denial is baseless. If he says yes, he admits to knowing something.
This exposes a contradiction: to deny knowledge, he must claim knowledge.
4
Consider the role of pain
Maturidi mentions inflicting pain to show that the denier would acknowledge the reality of pain, which is a sensory experience. This proves he knows sensory knowledge.
This is a reductio ad absurdum, not a general method.
5
Conclude the refutation's goal
The aim is to embarrass the denier and force him to abandon his obstinacy by confronting the self-evident nature of sensory knowledge.

Source Text

Empirical perception is what the senses fall upon, and it is the foundation which possesses knowledge that has no opposite in ignorance. Whoever says its opposite from ignorance is called a denier, a stubborn disputant whom the nature of beasts refuses to have such a rank; for each of them knows what constitutes its survival and perishing, and what it finds pleasurable and painful. Yet this person denies that. It is agreed that one does not debate with someone who holds such a statement, for his denial is not established, nor his presence in himself. Debate is about the quiddity (mā'iyyah) or existence (hastiyyah) of a thing, and he is for it. For every push there is a pusher, but he mixes things up. So it is said to him: 'Do you know that you are denying?' If he says 'No,' his denial is invalidated. If he affirms his denial, then by what he pushes he becomes a pusher of his own push. He is pained, and pained with severe pain. Whoever says 'Yes' cuts off his limbs to abandon his obstinacy, for we know that he knows empirical perception because it is necessary knowledge (ḍarūrah). But he is obstinate in his statement. The treatment of such a person is what I mentioned, so that he may be frightened and annoyed, and be met with obstinacy so that his cover is torn. ?

The Two Types of Reports and the Self-Contradiction of Denying Reports
Maturidi classifies reports into two types and refutes the denier by showing self-contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of reports and why denying reports altogether is logically self-contradictory.

Core Idea
Denial of a report is itself a report — you cannot escape it.

Maturidi begins by classifying reports into two types. But before detailing them, he highlights a profound contradiction: anyone who denies all reports is caught in a logical trap, because their own denial is a report. This lesson explores that paradox and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the claim that the denier's denial is itself a report.
This is the key logical move.
2
Identify the contradiction
Maturidi argues that the denier, by denying reports, uses a report (his denial). Thus he contradicts himself. Identify this argument in the text.
3
Reflect on the implications of denial
If the denier's denial implies ignorance of his own lineage, name, essence, etc., then he cannot claim to know anything about reports. Think about why this is absurd.
This shows the self-destructive nature of extreme skepticism.
4
Connect to broader epistemology
Consider how this argument strengthens the case for accepting reports as a valid source of knowledge alongside sensory perception.
This sets the stage for the rest of the lesson series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأخبار نوعان ، من أذكر جملته لحق بالفريق الأول ؛ لأنه أنكر إنكاره ؛ إذ إنكاره خبر ، فيصير منكرا عند إنكاره -- إنكاره ، ما فيه جهل نسبه واسمه ومائيته واسم جوهره واسم كل شيء ، فيجب به جهل أن يُخبر عن شيء عاينه إذا خبر به ، فكيف يبلغ فكيف يبلغ هو إلى مع محسوس وعجزه عن العلم بما يبلغه مما غاب عنه ، أو متى يعلم ما به معاشه / وغذاؤه ، وكل ذلك يصل إليه بالخبر ، مع ما فيه الكفران بعظيم نعم الله عليه ، وبأصل ما محمد هو به ، و بما فضل به على البهائم من النطق بالسمع ، وذلك نهاية المكابرة . قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم لا يوصل بهما إلى إدراك المحاسن والمساوئ التي لا يعمل العقول على الإحاطة [بها] إلا باستعمال الألسن بالتكلم بها وإدناء السمع إليها . وحق مناظرة هذا وإن كانت مناظرته سفها – فيمازج أيضا ، فنقول له عند إنكاره الخبر : ما تقول ؟ فإن عاد إليه فاعلم أنه قبل خبرك حيث عاد إلى قوله وهو استعادتك الخبر ، وإن لم يعد إليه كفيت شره وحمدت الله وضحكت ، منه ومثله لمن ينكر العيان ، تقول له : ما تقول ؟ فإن عاد إليه ظهر لك أنه يعلمه ، ولكنه يتعنت ، وإن لم يعد إليه ، كفيت شره ، وشكرت الله تعالى على ما ألهمك ، أو تضربه وتؤلم ، فإنه لا يقدر أن يضجر أو يقابلك بالعتاب لما لا يحتمل ذلك إلا بتسمية فعلك ، وذلك يعرف بالخير ، وقد أنكره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم إذ قد لزم قبول الأخبار بضرورة العقل لزم قبول أخبار الرسل ؛ إذ لا من تخبرهم خبرهم بما معهم من الآيات الموضحة صدقهم ؛ إذ لا يوجد خبر يطمئن إليه القلب مما بينا من المعارف التى يصير منكر ذلك متعنتا بضرورة العقل - أوضح صدقا من أخبار الرسل صلوات الله عليهم ، فمن أنكر ذلك فهو أحق من يقضى عليه بالتعنت والمكابرة . ثم الأخبار التي تنتهى إلينا من الرسل تنتهى على السن من يُحتمل منهم الغلط والكذب ؛ إذ ليس معهم دليل الصدق ولا برهان العصمة ، فحق مثله النظر فيه . فإن كان مثله مما لا يوجد
The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Reports are of two types. Whoever rejects the totality of report joins the first group, because he is denying his own denial, since his denial is a report. So he becomes a denier by his denial. His denial contains ignorance of his lineage, his name, his essence (mā'iyyah), the name of his substance, and the name of everything. So it necessitates ignorance that he can report about something he witnessed when he reports about it. How can he reach a sensory thing while being incapable of knowing what reaches him from what is absent? Or when does he know what his livelihood and nourishment are? All of that reaches him through report. This involves ingratitude for God's great blessings, the foundation of Muhammad's mission, and what distinguishes him from beasts: speech and hearing. That is the ultimate obstinacy. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Moreover, one cannot attain through these two [sensory perception and report] the perception of goods and evils that minds cannot encompass except by using tongues to speak and inclining ears to listen. The proper way to debate this person—even though his debate is foolish—is to mix with him and say to him when he denies the report: 'What do you say?' If he returns to it, know that he has accepted your report because he has reverted to his statement, which is your repetition of the report. If he does not return to it, you are spared his evil, praise God, and laugh. Similarly, regarding the denier of sensory perception, you say to him: 'What do you say?' If he returns to it, it becomes clear that he knows it but is obstinate. If he does not return to it, you are spared his evil, thank God for what He inspired you, or hit and pain him, for he cannot be annoyed or respond with reproach except by naming your action, and that is known through report, which he has denied. There is no power except with God. Then, since the acceptance of reports is necessary by the necessity of the intellect, the acceptance of the reports of the messengers becomes necessary. For there is none whose report gives more tranquil assurance to the heart than the reports of the messengers, with the signs that clarify their truthfulness. Among the knowledge we have mentioned, the denier of that becomes obstinate by the necessity of the intellect. Whoever denies that is most deserving of being judged as obstinate and stubborn. Furthermore, the reports that reach us from the messengers come through tongues where error and falsehood are possible, since there is no evidence of truthfulness or proof of infallibility with them. So it is right to investigate them. If such [investigation] does not exist...
Source pages p. 606

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the sources of knowledge, focusing on reports (al-akhbār). The author states that reports are of two types—likely referring to a further division, but the text emphasizes that whoever denies reports altogether falls into self-contradiction because his denial itself is a report. The refutation method for the denier of reports is similar to that for the denier of sensory perception: ask him what he says; if he responds, he implicitly accepts the report. The passage then argues that since the intellect necessarily accepts reports (because we depend on them for daily life and knowledge of absent matters), we must also accept the reports of prophets. The prophetic reports come with clear signs (āyāt) demonstrating their truthfulness, and no other reports give more certainty. The author concludes by noting that reports from prophets are transmitted through human channels where error and falsehood are possible, so they require investigation (naẓar) to confirm their truth. This sets the stage for the need for rational examination of prophetic claims.

Key Terms
الأخبار
النوعان
التعنت
المكابرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying all reports is a rational stance, like skepticism about history.
Correction: Maturidi shows that denial of reports is self-refuting because the denial itself is a report; it is not a tenable position.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is the denier of reports caught in a contradiction?
A) Because he cannot prove his denial without using a report.
B) Because his denial is itself a report.
C) Because he relies on reports for his daily life.
D) Because he uses sensory perception to deny reports.

Key Takeaway
Denying all reports is logically impossible because the denial itself is a report, making the position self-contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reject a report that challenges your comfort or ego, are you aware that your rejection itself is a report about your own state? What does that report say about you?
?
The denier becomes ignorant of his own lineage and essence. What authentic reports from Allah or His messenger are you currently denying, and how has that denial affected your knowledge of who you truly are?
?
If denying all reports is self-contradictory, could it be that your selective denial of certain truths (about yourself, your faith, or the world) is also a contradiction? Which uncomfortable truth are you most refusing to hear today?

The Rational Necessity of Reports and the Method of Refuting Deniers
Maturidi argues for the rational necessity of reports and provides a method to refute deniers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reports are rationally necessary for knowledge and daily life, and how to refute someone who stubbornly denies reports or sensory perception.

Core Idea
We cannot live without reports; even our food and drink are known through them.

Maturidi moves from the self-contradiction of total denial to a positive argument: reports are indispensable for everyday life, from livelihood to nutrition. He then provides a practical method for debating someone who refuses to accept reports or sensory evidence, involving a simple question: 'What do you say?'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the necessity of reports
Read that part of the Arabic where Maturidi asks rhetorical questions about how we know our sustenance. Notice the argument from common experience.
This is an inductive argument.
2
Understand the refutation method
Maturidi suggests a dialectical approach: ask the denier 'What do you say?' If he responds, he has accepted a report. If not, you are free from his harm. Compare this to the parallel argument for sensory perception.
It is a practical way to expose inconsistency.
3
Apply the method to a hypothetical
Imagine someone denies that eating bread nourishes you because it is based on reports. How would you use the 'What do you say?' technique?
This helps internalize the refutation.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
If reports are necessary for basic survival, then rejecting them is not just wrong but harmful and ungrateful to God.
Maturidi emphasizes ingratitude (kufran) for God's blessings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ رحمه الله : والأخبار نوعان ، من أذكر جملته لحق بالفريق الأول ؛ لأنه أنكر إنكاره ؛ إذ إنكاره خبر ، فيصير منكرا عند إنكاره -- إنكاره ، ما فيه جهل نسبه واسمه ومائيته واسم جوهره واسم كل شيء ، فيجب به جهل أن يُخبر عن شيء عاينه إذا خبر به ، فكيف يبلغ فكيف يبلغ هو إلى مع محسوس وعجزه عن العلم بما يبلغه مما غاب عنه ، أو متى يعلم ما به معاشه / وغذاؤه ، وكل ذلك يصل إليه بالخبر ، مع ما فيه الكفران بعظيم نعم الله عليه ، وبأصل ما محمد هو به ، و بما فضل به على البهائم من النطق بالسمع ، وذلك نهاية المكابرة . قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم لا يوصل بهما إلى إدراك المحاسن والمساوئ التي لا يعمل العقول على الإحاطة [بها] إلا باستعمال الألسن بالتكلم بها وإدناء السمع إليها . وحق مناظرة هذا وإن كانت مناظرته سفها – فيمازج أيضا ، فنقول له عند إنكاره الخبر : ما تقول ؟ فإن عاد إليه فاعلم أنه قبل خبرك حيث عاد إلى قوله وهو استعادتك الخبر ، وإن لم يعد إليه كفيت شره وحمدت الله وضحكت ، منه ومثله لمن ينكر العيان ، تقول له : ما تقول ؟ فإن عاد إليه ظهر لك أنه يعلمه ، ولكنه يتعنت ، وإن لم يعد إليه ، كفيت شره ، وشكرت الله تعالى على ما ألهمك ، أو تضربه وتؤلم ، فإنه لا يقدر أن يضجر أو يقابلك بالعتاب لما لا يحتمل ذلك إلا بتسمية فعلك ، وذلك يعرف بالخير ، وقد أنكره ، ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم إذ قد لزم قبول الأخبار بضرورة العقل لزم قبول أخبار الرسل ؛ إذ لا من تخبرهم خبرهم بما معهم من الآيات الموضحة صدقهم ؛ إذ لا يوجد خبر يطمئن إليه القلب مما بينا من المعارف التى يصير منكر ذلك متعنتا بضرورة العقل - أوضح صدقا من أخبار الرسل صلوات الله عليهم ، فمن أنكر ذلك فهو أحق من يقضى عليه بالتعنت والمكابرة . ثم الأخبار التي تنتهى إلينا من الرسل تنتهى على السن من يُحتمل منهم الغلط والكذب ؛ إذ ليس معهم دليل الصدق ولا برهان العصمة ، فحق مثله النظر فيه . فإن كان مثله مما لا يوجد
The Shaykh (may God have mercy on him) said: Reports are of two types. Whoever rejects the totality of report joins the first group, because he is denying his own denial, since his denial is a report. So he becomes a denier by his denial. His denial contains ignorance of his lineage, his name, his essence (mā'iyyah), the name of his substance, and the name of everything. So it necessitates ignorance that he can report about something he witnessed when he reports about it. How can he reach a sensory thing while being incapable of knowing what reaches him from what is absent? Or when does he know what his livelihood and nourishment are? All of that reaches him through report. This involves ingratitude for God's great blessings, the foundation of Muhammad's mission, and what distinguishes him from beasts: speech and hearing. That is the ultimate obstinacy. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: Moreover, one cannot attain through these two [sensory perception and report] the perception of goods and evils that minds cannot encompass except by using tongues to speak and inclining ears to listen. The proper way to debate this person—even though his debate is foolish—is to mix with him and say to him when he denies the report: 'What do you say?' If he returns to it, know that he has accepted your report because he has reverted to his statement, which is your repetition of the report. If he does not return to it, you are spared his evil, praise God, and laugh. Similarly, regarding the denier of sensory perception, you say to him: 'What do you say?' If he returns to it, it becomes clear that he knows it but is obstinate. If he does not return to it, you are spared his evil, thank God for what He inspired you, or hit and pain him, for he cannot be annoyed or respond with reproach except by naming your action, and that is known through report, which he has denied. There is no power except with God. Then, since the acceptance of reports is necessary by the necessity of the intellect, the acceptance of the reports of the messengers becomes necessary. For there is none whose report gives more tranquil assurance to the heart than the reports of the messengers, with the signs that clarify their truthfulness. Among the knowledge we have mentioned, the denier of that becomes obstinate by the necessity of the intellect. Whoever denies that is most deserving of being judged as obstinate and stubborn. Furthermore, the reports that reach us from the messengers come through tongues where error and falsehood are possible, since there is no evidence of truthfulness or proof of infallibility with them. So it is right to investigate them. If such [investigation] does not exist...

The author states that reports are of two types—likely referring to a further division, but the text emphasizes that whoever denies reports altogether falls into self-contradiction because his denial itself is a report. B) 'What do you say?'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you rejected a truth from the Quran or Sunnah because it didn't align with your personal experience? In that moment, were you not relying on reports for most of what you know—like your own name, history, and even the food you ate today? What would it cost you to honestly answer the question 'What do you say?' to Allah's report?
?
Maturidi says that even your food and drink reach you through report. Do you live with a constant awareness that your daily existence depends on the trustworthiness of others? How does this awareness change your gratitude to Allah for providing through a chain of witnesses, and how does it challenge your reluctance to trust the witness of the prophets?
?
Imagine you are in a debate with someone who denies the necessity of revelation. You ask 'What do you say?' and they remain silent. Would you be satisfied with that outcome? Or would you, like Maturidi, recognize that silence is a tacit admission, and that your own heart often remains silent when confronted with truth? What are you avoiding by remaining silent about the reports of Allah?
